<td style="width:60%; max-height:1px; border-top:2px solid transparent; border-left:1px solid transparent;">

<p><b>Propane</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;p&#39; in &#39;pie&#39;">p</span><span title="&#39;r&#39; in &#39;rye&#39;">r</span><span title="/oʊ/: &#39;o&#39; in &#39;code&#39;">oʊ</span><span title="&#39;p&#39; in &#39;pie&#39;">p</span><span title="/eɪ/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;face&#39;">eɪ</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span></span>/</a></span></span>) is a three-<a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a> <a href="/wiki/Alkane" title="Alkane">alkane</a> with the molecular formula C<sub>3</sub>H<sub>8</sub>. It is a gas at <a href="/wiki/Standard_temperature_and_pressure" class="mw-redirect" title="Standard temperature and pressure">standard temperature and pressure</a>, but compressible to a transportable liquid. A <a href="/wiki/By-product" title="By-product">by-product</a> of <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a> processing and <a href="/wiki/Petroleum" title="Petroleum">petroleum</a> refining, it is commonly used as a <a href="/wiki/Fuel" title="Fuel">fuel</a>. Propane is one of a group of <a href="/wiki/Liquefied_petroleum_gas" title="Liquefied petroleum gas">liquefied petroleum gases</a> (LP gases). The others include <a href="/wiki/Butane" title="Butane">butane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Propylene" class="mw-redirect" title="Propylene">propylene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Butadiene" class="mw-redirect" title="Butadiene">butadiene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Butene" title="Butene">butylene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isobutylene" title="Isobutylene">isobutylene</a>, and mixtures thereof.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Propane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Propane was discovered by the French chemist <a href="/wiki/Marcellin_Berthelot" title="Marcellin Berthelot">Marcellin Berthelot</a> in 1857.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> It was first identified as a volatile component in <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a> by <a href="/wiki/Walter_O._Snelling" title="Walter O. Snelling">Walter O. Snelling</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Bureau_of_Mines" title="United States Bureau of Mines">U.S. Bureau of Mines</a> in 1910. Although the compound was known long before this, Snelling's work was the beginning of the propane industry in the United States. The volatility of these lighter <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbons" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrocarbons">hydrocarbons</a> caused them to be known as "wild" because of the high vapor pressures of unrefined gasoline. On March 31, 1912, <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> reported on Snelling's work with liquefied gas, saying "a <a href="/wiki/Gas_cylinder" title="Gas cylinder">steel bottle</a> will carry enough <a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">gas</a> to light an ordinary home for three weeks".<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It was during this time that Snelling, in cooperation with Frank P. Peterson, Chester Kerr, and Arthur Kerr, created ways to liquefy the LP gases during the refining of natural gasoline. Together, they established American Gasol Co., the first commercial marketer of propane. Snelling had produced relatively pure propane by 1911, and on March 25, 1913, his method of processing and producing LP gases was issued patent #1,056,845.<sup id="cite_ref-history_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-history-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> A separate method of producing LP gas through compression was created by Frank Peterson and its patent granted on July 2, 1912.<sup id="cite_ref-lpga_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lpga-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The 1920s saw increased production of LP gas, with the first year of recorded production totaling 223,000 US gallons (840&#160;m<sup>3</sup>) in 1922. In 1927, annual marketed LP gas production reached 1&#160;million US gallons (3,800&#160;m<sup>3</sup>), and by 1935, the annual sales of LP gas had reached 56&#160;million US gallons (210,000&#160;m<sup>3</sup>). Major industry developments in the 1930s included the introduction of railroad tank car transport, gas odorization, and the construction of local bottle-filling plants. The year 1945 marked the first year that annual LP gas sales reached a billion gallons. By 1947, 62% of all U.S. homes had been equipped with either natural gas or propane for cooking.<sup id="cite_ref-history_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-history-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1950, 1,000 propane-fueled buses were ordered by the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Transit_Authority" title="Chicago Transit Authority">Chicago Transit Authority</a>, and by 1958, sales in the U.S. had reached 7&#160;billion US gallons (26,000,000&#160;m<sup>3</sup>) annually. In 2004, it was reported to be a growing $8-billion to $10-billion industry with over 15&#160;billion US gallons (57,000,000&#160;m<sup>3</sup>) of propane being used annually in the U.S.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The "<a href="/wiki/Prop-" title="Prop-">prop-</a>" root found in "propane" and names of other compounds with three-carbon chains was derived from "<a href="/wiki/Propionic_acid" title="Propionic acid">propionic acid</a>",<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> which in turn was named after the Greek words protos (meaning first) and pion (fat).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sources">Sources</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Propane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Sources">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Propane is produced as a <a href="/wiki/By-product" title="By-product">by-product</a> of two other processes, <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas_processing" class="mw-redirect" title="Natural gas processing">natural gas processing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oil_refinery" title="Oil refinery">petroleum refining</a>. The processing of natural gas involves removal of <a href="/wiki/Butane" title="Butane">butane</a>, propane, and large amounts of <a href="/wiki/Ethane" title="Ethane">ethane</a> from the raw gas, in order to prevent condensation of these volatiles in natural gas pipelines. Additionally, oil refineries produce some propane as a by-product of <a href="/wiki/Cracking_(chemistry)" title="Cracking (chemistry)">cracking</a> petroleum into gasoline or heating oil.
</p><p>The supply of propane cannot easily be adjusted to meet increased demand, because of the by-product nature of propane production. About 90% of U.S. propane is domestically produced.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The United States imports about 10% of the propane consumed each year, with about 70% of that coming from Canada via pipeline and rail. The remaining 30% of imported propane comes to the United States from other sources via ocean transport.
</p><p>After it is separated from the crude oil, North American propane is stored in huge <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas_storage#Salt_formation" title="Natural gas storage">salt caverns</a>. Examples of these are <a href="/wiki/Fort_Saskatchewan" title="Fort Saskatchewan">Fort Saskatchewan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alberta" title="Alberta">Alberta</a>; <a href="/wiki/Mont_Belvieu,_Texas" title="Mont Belvieu, Texas">Mont Belvieu, Texas</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Conway,_Kansas" title="Conway, Kansas">Conway, Kansas</a>. These salt caverns were hollowed out in the 1940s,<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> and they can store 80,000,000 barrels (13,000,000&#160;m<sup>3</sup>) or more of propane. When the propane is needed, much of it is shipped by pipelines to other areas of the United States. The North American standard grade of automotive use propane is rated HD 5. HD 5 grade has a maximum of 5 percent butane, but propane sold in Europe, has a max allowable amount of butane of 30 percent, meaning it's not the same fuel as HD 5. The LPG used as auto fuel and cooking gas in Asia and Australia, also has a very high content of butane. Propane is also shipped by truck, ship, barge, and railway to many U.S. areas.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Propane can also be produced as a <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Properties_and_reactions">Properties and reactions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Propane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Properties and reactions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Propane_flame_contours-en.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Propane_flame_contours-en.svg/220px-Propane_flame_contours-en.svg.png" width="220" height="350" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Propane_flame_contours-en.svg/330px-Propane_flame_contours-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Propane_flame_contours-en.svg/440px-Propane_flame_contours-en.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="722" data-file-height="1147" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Propane_flame_contours-en.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Pyrometry" class="mw-redirect" title="Pyrometry">Pyrometry</a> of a propane flame using thin-filament velocimetry.  The hottest parts of the flame are in a hollow cone-shaped area near its base and pointing upward. <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:red; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;&gt;1,750&#160;K (1,480&#160;°C)</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:orange; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;1,700&#160;K (1,430&#160;°C)</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:yellow; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;1,600&#160;K (1,330&#160;°C)</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:lime; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;1,350&#160;K (1,080&#160;°C)</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:cyan; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;1,100&#160;K (830&#160;°C)</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:blue; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;875&#160;K (602&#160;°C)</div> <div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color:purple; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;750&#160;K (477&#160;°C)</div></div></div></div>
<p>Propane undergoes <a href="/wiki/Combustion" title="Combustion">combustion</a> reactions in a similar fashion to other <a href="/wiki/Alkane" title="Alkane">alkanes</a>. In the presence of excess oxygen, propane burns to form water and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>.<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\displaystyle {\ce {C3H8 + 5O2 -&gt; 3CO2 + 4H2O + heat}}}">

</math></div><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9dd914f5be282f20d7eb79fb3233b9344923cfc4" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:37.333ex; height:2.843ex;" alt="{\displaystyle {\ce {C3H8 + 2O2 -&gt; 3C + 4H2O + heat}}}"/></div> Complete combustion of propane produces about 50 MJ/kg of heat.<sup id="cite_ref-nist_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nist-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Propane combustion is much cleaner than unleaded gasoline combustion, and cleaner than natural gas combustion, because of the extremely high hydrogen content in propane. Propane burns hotter than home heating oil or diesel fuel because of the very high hydrogen content. Natural gas, known as methane shipped to your home or office, doesn't have the high hydrogen content of propane as used in North America.  The presence of <a href="/wiki/Carbon%E2%80%93carbon_bond" title="Carbon–carbon bond">C–C bonds</a>, plus the multiple bonds of <a href="/wiki/Propylene" class="mw-redirect" title="Propylene">propylene</a> and <a href="/wiki/Butene" title="Butene">butylene</a>, create organic exhausts besides carbon dioxide and water vapor during typical combustion. These bonds also cause propane to burn with a visible flame.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_content">Energy content</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Propane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Energy content">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Enthalpy" title="Enthalpy">enthalpy</a> of combustion of propane gas where all products return to standard state, for example where water returns to its liquid state at standard temperature (known as higher heating value), is (2219.2 ± 0.5) kJ/mol, or (50.33 ± 0.01) MJ/kg.<sup id="cite_ref-nist_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nist-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
The enthalpy of combustion of propane gas where products do not return to standard state, for example where the hot gases including water vapor exit a chimney, (known as lower heating value) is −2043.455 kJ/mol.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>  The lower heat value is the amount of heat available from burning the substance where the combustion products are vented to the atmosphere.  For example, the heat from a fireplace when the flue is open. Propane used in North America as auto fuel and forklift uses, is rated as HD 5 grade and it has an octane rating of 104.5 R+M. The R means Research octane and the M means Motor octane. Both the R+M must be added together to get the average of those two different lab tests. EG: Methanol is 99 octane while ethanol is 99.5 octane, as a comparison, CNG natural gas is rated at 120 octane, so run your engines with 15 to one compression ratios and have fun. 
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Density">Density</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Propane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Density">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The density of liquid propane at 25&#160;°C (77&#160;°F) is 0.493&#160;g/cm<sup>3</sup>, which is equivalent to 4.11 pounds per U.S. liquid gallon or 493&#160;g/L. Propane expands at 1.5% per 10&#160;°F. Thus, liquid propane has a density of approximately 4.2 pounds per gallon (504&#160;g/L) at 60&#160;°F (15.6&#160;°C).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses">Uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Propane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Propane is a popular choice for barbecues and portable stoves because the low <a href="/wiki/Boiling_point" title="Boiling point">boiling point</a> of −42&#160;°C (−44&#160;°F) makes it vaporize as soon as it is released from its pressurized container. Therefore, no <a href="/wiki/Carburetor" title="Carburetor">carburetor</a> or other vaporizing device is required; a simple metering nozzle suffices. Propane powers some locomotive diesel engines as a fuel added into the turbocharger to give much better combustion, <a href="/wiki/Bus" title="Bus">buses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Forklift" title="Forklift">forklifts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taxicab" title="Taxicab">taxis</a>, outboard boat motors, and <a href="/wiki/Ice_resurfacer" title="Ice resurfacer">ice resurfacing machines</a> and is used for heat and cooking in <a href="/wiki/Recreational_vehicle" title="Recreational vehicle">recreational vehicles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Campervan" title="Campervan">campers</a>. 
</p><p>Since it can be transported easily, it is a popular fuel for home heat and backup electrical generation in sparsely populated areas that do not have natural gas pipelines. Many heavy duty highway trucks use propane as a boost, where it is added through the turbocharger, to mix with the diesel fuel droplets. The very high hydrogen content of the propane droplets, helps the diesel fuel to burn hotter and therefore more complete. This means more torque, more horsepower and cleaner exhaust. It is normal for a 7 liter medium duty diesel truck engine to increase fuel mileage by 20 to 33 percent when they use a propane boost system. It's cheaper because propane is much cheaper than diesel fuel. The longer distance a cross country trucker can travel on a full load of fuel, combined diesel and propane, means he can keep within his federal hours of work rules with two fewer fuel stops across the country. 
</p><p>Truckers, tractor pulling competitions and farmers have been using a propane boost system for over 40 years in North America. International ships can use propane fumes that evaporate from ocean going ships that transport LPG, because as the sun warms up the propane during the voyage, they catch the evaporating propane gas and feed it into the air intake system of the ships diesel engines. This reduces bunker fuel consumption and helps the exhaust fumes to put out less pollution. This is very critical for the 2020 year as there is an international agreement to use either propane or CNG natural gas to be a mandatory additive to the bunker fuel ( very heavy oil ) for all ships traveling the oceans starting in 2020.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Propane_tank_20lb.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Propane_tank_20lb.jpg/220px-Propane_tank_20lb.jpg" width="220" height="287" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Propane_tank_20lb.jpg/330px-Propane_tank_20lb.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Propane_tank_20lb.jpg/440px-Propane_tank_20lb.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1973" data-file-height="2575" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Propane_tank_20lb.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 20 lb (<span class="nowrap">9.1 kg</span>) steel propane cylinder.  This cylinder is fitted with an overfill prevention device (OPD) valve, as evidenced by the trilobular handwheel.</div></div></div>
<p>Propane is generally stored and transported in steel cylinders as a liquid with a vapor space above the liquid. The vapor pressure in the cylinder is a function of temperature. When gaseous propane is drawn at a high rate, the latent heat of vaporisation required to create the gas will cause the bottle to cool. (This is why water often condenses on the sides of the bottle and then freezes). In addition, the lightweight, high-<a href="/wiki/Octane_rating" title="Octane rating">octane</a> compounds vaporize before the heavier, low-octane ones. Thus, the ignition properties change as the cylinder empties. For these reasons, the liquid is often withdrawn using a dip tube. Propane is used as fuel in furnaces for heat, in cooking, as an energy source for water heaters, laundry dryers, <a href="/wiki/Barbecue" title="Barbecue">barbecues</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portable_stove" title="Portable stove">portable stoves</a>, and motor vehicles.
</p><p>Commercially available "propane" fuel, or <a href="/wiki/Liquefied_petroleum_gas" title="Liquefied petroleum gas">LPG</a>, is not pure. Typically in the United States and Canada, it is primarily propane (at least 90%), with the rest mostly <a href="/wiki/Ethane" title="Ethane">ethane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Propylene" class="mw-redirect" title="Propylene">propylene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Butane" title="Butane">butane</a>, and odorants including <a href="/wiki/Ethyl_mercaptan" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethyl mercaptan">ethyl mercaptan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> This is the HD-5 standard, (<b>H</b>eavy <b>D</b>uty-<b>5%</b> maximum allowable propylene content, and no more than 5% butanes and ethane) defined by the American Society for Testing and Materials by its <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://hd5propane.com/">Standard 1835</a> for internal combustion engines. Not all products labeled "LPG" conform to this standard however. In Mexico, for example, gas labeled "LPG" may consist of 60% propane and 40% butane. "The exact proportion of this combination varies by country, depending on international prices, on the availability of components and, especially, on the climatic conditions that favor LPG with higher butane content in warmer regions and propane in cold areas".<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Domestic_and_industrial_fuel">Domestic and industrial fuel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Propane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Domestic and industrial fuel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:150_gallon_Propane_Tank.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/150_gallon_Propane_Tank.jpg/170px-150_gallon_Propane_Tank.jpg" width="170" height="213" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/150_gallon_Propane_Tank.jpg/255px-150_gallon_Propane_Tank.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/150_gallon_Propane_Tank.jpg/340px-150_gallon_Propane_Tank.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1184" data-file-height="1480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:150_gallon_Propane_Tank.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Domestic spherical steel pressure vessel  with <a href="/wiki/Pressure_regulator" title="Pressure regulator">pressure regulator</a> for propane storage in the United States. This example was installed on this property in 1974.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:PropaneTruck2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/PropaneTruck2.jpg/220px-PropaneTruck2.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/PropaneTruck2.jpg/330px-PropaneTruck2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/PropaneTruck2.jpg/440px-PropaneTruck2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4928" data-file-height="3264" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:PropaneTruck2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A local delivery truck</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Propane_retail_06758.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Propane_retail_06758.JPG/220px-Propane_retail_06758.JPG" width="220" height="162" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Propane_retail_06758.JPG/330px-Propane_retail_06758.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Propane_retail_06758.JPG/440px-Propane_retail_06758.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2505" data-file-height="1849" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Propane_retail_06758.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Retail sale of propane in the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a></div></div></div>
<p>Propane use is growing rapidly in non-industrialized areas of the world. Propane has replaced many older other traditional fuel sources.
</p><p>North American industries using propane include glass makers, brick kilns, poultry farms and other industries that need portable heat.
</p><p>In rural areas of North America, as well as northern Australia, propane is used to heat livestock facilities, in grain dryers, and other heat-producing appliances. When used for heating or <a href="/wiki/Grain_drying" title="Grain drying">grain drying</a> it is usually stored in a large, permanently-placed cylinder which is recharged by a propane-delivery truck. As of  2014<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Propane&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, 6.2 million American households use propane as their primary heating fuel.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In North America, local delivery trucks with an average cylinder size of 3,000 US gallons (11,000&#160;L), fill up large cylinders that are permanently installed on the property, or other service trucks exchange empty cylinders of propane with filled cylinders. Large tractor-trailer trucks, with an average cylinder size of 10,000 US gallons (38,000&#160;L), transport the propane from the pipeline or refinery to the local bulk plant. The bobtail and transport are not unique to the North American market, though the practice is not as common elsewhere, and the vehicles are generally called <i>tankers</i>. In many countries, propane is delivered to consumers via small or medium-sized individual cylinders, while empty cylinders are removed for refilling at a central location.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Propene" title="Propene">Propene</a> (also called propylene) can be a contaminant of commercial propane. Propane containing too much propene is not suited for most vehicle fuels. HD-5 is a specification that establishes a maximum concentration of 5% propene in propane. Propane and other LP gas specifications are established in ASTM D-1835.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> All propane fuels include an <a href="/wiki/Aroma_compound" title="Aroma compound">odorant</a>, almost always <a href="/wiki/Ethanethiol" title="Ethanethiol">ethanethiol</a>, so that people can easily smell the gas in case of a leak. Propane as HD-5 was originally intended for use as vehicle fuel. HD-5 is currently being used in all propane applications.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Refrigeration">Refrigeration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Propane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Refrigeration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Propane is also instrumental in providing <a href="/wiki/Off-the-grid" title="Off-the-grid">off-the-grid</a> refrigeration, as the energy source for a <a href="/wiki/Gas_absorption_refrigerator" class="mw-redirect" title="Gas absorption refrigerator">gas absorption refrigerator</a> and is commonly used for camping and recreational vehicles.
In addition, blends of pure, dry "isopropane" (R-290a) (isobutane/propane mixtures) and <a href="/wiki/Isobutane" title="Isobutane">isobutane</a> (R-600a) can be used as the circulating refrigerant in suitably constructed compressor-based refrigeration. Compared to fluorocarbons, propane has a negligible <a href="/wiki/Ozone_depletion_potential" title="Ozone depletion potential">ozone depletion potential</a> and very low <a href="/wiki/Global_warming_potential" title="Global warming potential">global warming potential</a> (having a value of only 3.3 times the GWP of carbon dioxide) and can serve as a functional replacement for <a href="/wiki/Dichlorodifluoromethane" title="Dichlorodifluoromethane">R-12</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chlorodifluoromethane" title="Chlorodifluoromethane">R-22</a>, <a href="/wiki/1,1,1,2-Tetrafluoroethane" title="1,1,1,2-Tetrafluoroethane">R-134a</a>, and other <a href="/wiki/Chlorofluorocarbon" title="Chlorofluorocarbon">chlorofluorocarbon</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hydrofluorocarbon" title="Hydrofluorocarbon">hydrofluorocarbon</a> <a href="/wiki/Refrigerant" title="Refrigerant">refrigerants</a> in conventional stationary refrigeration and air conditioning systems.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Because its global warming effect is far less than current refrigerants, propane was chosen as one of five replacement refrigerants approved by the EPA in 2015, for use in systems specially designed to handle its flammability.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="In_motor_vehicles">In motor vehicles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Propane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: In motor vehicles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Such substitution is widely prohibited or discouraged in motor vehicle air conditioning systems, on the grounds that using <a href="/wiki/Flammability" class="mw-redirect" title="Flammability">flammable</a> hydrocarbons in systems originally designed to carry non-flammable refrigerant presents a significant risk of fire or explosion.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Vendors and advocates of hydrocarbon refrigerants argue against such bans on the grounds that there have been very few such incidents relative to the number of vehicle air conditioning systems filled with hydrocarbons.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Motor_fuel">Motor fuel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Propane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Motor fuel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Propane&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span>  <small><i>(October 2009)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Autogas" title="Autogas">Autogas</a></div>
<p>Propane is also being used increasingly for vehicle fuels. In the U.S., over 190,000 on-road vehicles use propane, and over 450,000 forklifts use it for power. It is the third most popular vehicle fuel in the world,<sup id="cite_ref-propane-as-fuel_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-propane-as-fuel-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> behind <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diesel_fuel" title="Diesel fuel">Diesel fuel</a>. In other parts of the world, propane used in vehicles is known as autogas. In 2007, approximately 13 million vehicles worldwide use autogas.<sup id="cite_ref-propane-as-fuel_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-propane-as-fuel-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The advantage of propane in cars is its liquid state at a moderate pressure. This allows fast refill times, affordable fuel cylinder construction, and price ranges typically just over half that of gasoline. Meanwhile, it is noticeably cleaner (both in handling, and in combustion), results in less engine wear (due to carbon deposits) without diluting engine oil (often extending oil-change intervals), and until recently was a relative bargain in North America. The <a href="/wiki/Octane_rating" title="Octane rating">octane rating</a> of propane is relatively high at 110. In the United States the propane fueling infrastructure is the most developed of all alternative vehicle fuels. Many converted vehicles have provisions for topping off from "barbecue bottles". Purpose-built vehicles are often in commercially owned fleets, and have private fueling facilities. A further saving for propane fuel vehicle operators, especially in fleets, is that pilferage is much more difficult than with gasoline or diesel fuels.
</p><p>Propane is also used as fuel for <a href="/wiki/Small_engine" title="Small engine">small engines</a>, especially those used indoors or in areas with insufficient fresh air and ventilation to carry away the more toxic exhaust of an engine running on gasoline or Diesel fuel. More recently, there have been lawn care products like <a href="/wiki/String_trimmer" title="String trimmer">string trimmers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lawn_mowers" class="mw-redirect" title="Lawn mowers">lawn mowers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Leaf_blowers" class="mw-redirect" title="Leaf blowers">leaf blowers</a> intended for outdoor use, but fueled by propane in order to reduce <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">air pollution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Improvised_explosive_devices">Improvised explosive devices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Propane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Improvised explosive devices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Propane and propane cylinders have been used as <a href="/wiki/Improvised_explosive_device" title="Improvised explosive device">improvised explosive devices</a> in attacks and attempted attacks against schools and terrorist targets such as the <a href="/wiki/Columbine_High_School_massacre" title="Columbine High School massacre">Columbine High School massacre</a>, <a href="/wiki/2012_Brindisi_school_bombing" title="2012 Brindisi school bombing">2012 Brindisi school bombing</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Discovery_Communications_headquarters_hostage_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="Discovery Communications headquarters hostage crisis">Discovery Communications headquarters hostage crisis</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Car_bomb" title="Car bomb">car bombs</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_uses">Other uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Propane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Other uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>Propane is the primary flammable gas in <a href="/wiki/Blowtorch" title="Blowtorch">blowtorches</a> for <a href="/wiki/Soldering" title="Soldering">soldering</a>.</li>
<li>Propane is used as a feedstock for the production of base <a href="/wiki/Petrochemical" title="Petrochemical">petrochemicals</a> in <a href="/wiki/Steam_cracking" class="mw-redirect" title="Steam cracking">steam cracking</a>.</li>
<li>Propane is the primary fuel for <a href="/wiki/Hot_air_balloon" title="Hot air balloon">hot air balloons</a>.</li>
<li>It is used in semiconductor manufacture to deposit <a href="/wiki/Silicon_carbide" title="Silicon carbide">silicon carbide</a>.</li>
<li>Propane is commonly used in theme parks and in the movie industry as an inexpensive, high-energy fuel for explosions and other special effects.</li>
<li>Propane is used as a propellant, relying on the expansion of the gas to fire the projectile. It does not ignite the gas. The use of a liquefied gas gives more shots per cylinder, compared to a compressed gas.</li>
<li>Propane is used as a propellant for many household aerosol sprays, including shaving creams and air fresheners.</li>
<li>Propane is a promising feedstock for the production of propylene<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> and acrylic acid.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PhD_Thesis_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PhD_Thesis-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Hazards">Hazards</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Propane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Hazards">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Propane is a simple <a href="/wiki/Asphyxia" title="Asphyxia">asphyxiant</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-cdc_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cdc-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> Unlike <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a>, propane is denser than air. It may accumulate in low spaces and near the floor. When abused as an <a href="/wiki/Intoxicative_inhalant" class="mw-redirect" title="Intoxicative inhalant">inhalant</a>, it may cause <a href="/wiki/Hypoxia_(medical)" title="Hypoxia (medical)">hypoxia</a> (lack of oxygen), <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_failure" class="mw-redirect" title="Cardiac failure">cardiac failure</a> or <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_arrest" title="Cardiac arrest">cardiac arrest</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-gdcada.org_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gdcada.org-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Propane has low <a href="/wiki/Toxicity" title="Toxicity">toxicity</a> since it is not readily absorbed and is not <a href="/wiki/Biological_activity" title="Biological activity">biologically active</a>. Commonly stored under pressure at room temperature, propane and its mixtures will <a href="/wiki/Flash_evaporation" title="Flash evaporation">flash evaporate</a> at atmospheric pressure and cool well below the freezing point of water. The cold gas, which appears white due to moisture condensing from the air, may cause frostbite.
</p><p>Propane is denser than air. If a leak in a propane fuel system occurs, the gas will have a tendency to sink into any enclosed area and thus poses a risk of explosion and fire. The typical scenario is a leaking cylinder stored in a basement; the propane leak drifts across the floor to the pilot light on the furnace or water heater, and results in an explosion or fire. This property makes propane generally unsuitable as a fuel for boats.
</p><p>One hazard associated with propane storage and transport is known as a BLEVE or <a href="/wiki/Boiling_liquid_expanding_vapor_explosion" title="Boiling liquid expanding vapor explosion">boiling liquid expanding vapor explosion</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Kingman_Explosion" class="mw-redirect" title="Kingman Explosion">Kingman Explosion</a> involved a railroad tank car in Kingman, Arizona in 1973 during a propane transfer. The fire and subsequent explosions resulted in twelve fatalities and numerous injuries.<sup id="cite_ref-Kingman_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kingman-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparison_with_natural_gas">Comparison with natural gas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Propane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Comparison with natural gas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Propane is bought and stored in a liquid form (LPG), and thus fuel energy can be stored in a relatively small space. Compressed natural gas (CNG), largely methane, is another gas used as fuel, but it cannot be liquefied by compression at normal temperatures, as these are well above its <a href="/wiki/Critical_temperature" class="mw-redirect" title="Critical temperature">critical temperature</a>. As a gas, very high pressure is required to store useful quantities. This poses the hazard that, in an accident, just as with any compressed gas cylinder (such as a CO<sub>2</sub> cylinder used for a soda concession) a CNG cylinder may burst with great force, or leak rapidly enough to become a self-propelled missile. Therefore, CNG is much less efficient to store, due to the large cylinder volume required. An alternative means of storing natural gas is as a <a href="/wiki/Cryogenics" title="Cryogenics">cryogenic</a> liquid in an insulated container as liquefied natural gas (LNG). This form of storage is at low pressure and is around 3.5 times as efficient as storing it as CNG. Unlike propane, if a spill occurs, CNG will evaporate and dissipate harmlessly because it is lighter than air. Propane is much more commonly used to fuel vehicles than is natural gas, because the equipment required costs less. Propane requires just 1,220 kilopascals (177&#160;psi) of pressure to keep it liquid at 37.8&#160;°C (100&#160;°F).<sup id="cite_ref-Vapor_Pressure_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vapor_Pressure-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Retail_cost">Retail cost</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Propane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Retail cost">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-globalize" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="Globe icon." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Ambox_globe_content.svg/48px-Ambox_globe_content.svg.png" width="48" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Ambox_globe_content.svg/73px-Ambox_globe_content.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Ambox_globe_content.svg/97px-Ambox_globe_content.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="350" data-file-height="290" /></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">The examples and perspective in this article <b>deal primarily with the United States and do not represent a <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:WikiProject_Countering_systemic_bias" title="Wikipedia:WikiProject Countering systemic bias">worldwide view</a> of the subject</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> You may <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Propane&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a>, discuss the issue on the <a href="/wiki/Talk:Propane" title="Talk:Propane">talk page</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Article_wizard" title="Wikipedia:Article wizard">create a new article</a>, as appropriate.</span>  <small><i>(August 2017)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Propane&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As of  October&#160;2013<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Propane&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, the retail cost of propane was approximately $2.37 per gallon, or roughly $25.95 per 1 million BTUs.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> This means that filling a 500-gallon propane tank, which is what households that use propane as their main source of energy usually require, costs $948 (80% of 500 gallons or 400 gallons), a 7.5% increase on the 2012–2013 winter season average US price.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> However, propane costs per gallon change significantly from one state to another: the Energy Information Administration (EIA) quotes a $2.995 per gallon average on the East Coast for October 2013,<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> while the figure for the Midwest was $1.860 for the same period.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As of  December&#160;2015<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Propane&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup> the propane retail cost was approximately $1.97 per gallon.<sup id="cite_ref-EIAdecember2015_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EIAdecember2015-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> This means that filling a 500-gallon propane tank to 80% capacity costs $788, a 16.9% decrease or $160 less from the November 2013 quote in this section. Similar regional differences in prices are present with the December 2015 EIA figure for the East Coast at $2.67 per gallon and the Midwest at $1.43 per gallon.<sup id="cite_ref-EIAdecember2015_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EIAdecember2015-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As of  August&#160;2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Propane&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup> the average US propane retail cost was approximately $2.48 per gallon. The wholesale price of propane in the U.S. always drops in the summer as most homes don't require it for home heating. The wholesale price of propane in the summer of 2018 has been between 86 cents to 96 cents per U.S. gallon, based on a truckload or railway car load. The daily propane prices are posted at the website Barrons dot com under market Data and under Cash Prices every day about 9 pm for that day's propane wholesale trade. The price for home heating is exactly double that price, so at 95 cents per gallon wholesale, that would mean a home delivered price of $1.90 per gallon if you order 500 gallons at a time. Prices in the midwest are always cheaper than California. Prices for home delivery always go up near the end of August or the first few days of September when people start ordering their home tanks to be filled.  <sup id="cite_ref-EIAaugust2018_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EIAaugust2018-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
